   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Concentrated solar power
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Not to be confused with
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">
       concentrator photovoltaics
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_(2).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1123" data-file-width="1500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%282%29.jpg/220px-Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%282%29.jpg/330px-Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%282%29.jpg/440px-Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The three towers of the
        <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
         Ivanpah Solar Power Facility
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Plant_kl.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="1008" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Solar_Plant_kl.jpg/220px-Solar_Plant_kl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Solar_Plant_kl.jpg/330px-Solar_Plant_kl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Solar_Plant_kl.jpg/440px-Solar_Plant_kl.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Plant_kl.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Part of the 354 MW
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEGS" title="SEGS">
         SEGS
        </a>
        solar complex in northern
        <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino_County,_California" title="San Bernardino County, California">
         San Bernardino County, California
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Concentrated solar power
      </b>
      (also called
      <b>
       concentrating solar power
      </b>
      ,
      <b>
       concentrated solar thermal
      </b>
      , and
      <b>
       CSP
      </b>
      ) systems generate
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
       solar power
      </a>
      by using mirrors or lenses to concentrate a large area of sunlight, or
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">
       solar thermal energy
      </a>
      , onto a small area.
      <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">
       Electricity
      </a>
      is generated when the concentrated light is converted to heat, which drives a
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine" title="Heat engine">
       heat engine
      </a>
      (usually a
      <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">
       steam turbine
      </a>
      ) connected to an electrical
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_generator" title="Power generator">
       power generator
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or powers a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermochemical" title="Thermochemical">
       thermochemical
      </a>
      reaction (experimental as of 2013
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunshine_to_Petrol_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sunshine_to_Petrol-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SunShot_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SunShot-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT41013_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYT41013-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      CSP is being widely commercialized and the CSP market saw about 740
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">
       megawatt
      </a>
      (MW) of generating capacity added between 2007 and the end of 2010. More than half of this (about 478 MW) was installed during 2010, bringing the global total to 1095 MW. Spain added 400 MW in 2010, taking the global lead with a total of 632 MW, while the US ended the year with 509 MW after adding 78 MW, including two fossil–CSP hybrid plants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saw2011_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saw2011-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Middle East is also ramping up their plans to install CSP based projects and as a part of that Plan,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shams_solar_power_station" title="Shams solar power station">
       Shams-I
      </a>
      which was the largest CSP Project in the world has been installed in Abu Dhabi, by
      <a href="/wiki/Masdar" title="Masdar">
       Masdar
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The largest CSP project in the world until January 2016 is
      <a href="/wiki/Ouarzazate_Solar_Power_Station" title="Ouarzazate Solar Power Station">
       Noor
      </a>
      in Morocco
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and global operational power stands at 4,705 MW.
     </p>
     <p>
      There is considerable academic and commercial interest internationally in a new form of CSP, called STEM, for off-grid applications to produce 24-hour industrial scale power for mining sites and remote communities in
      <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
       Italy
      </a>
      , other parts of Europe, Australia, Asia,
      <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">
       North Africa
      </a>
      and Latin America. STEM uses fluidized
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica_sand" title="Silica sand">
       silica sand
      </a>
      as a thermal storage and
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">
       heat transfer
      </a>
      medium for CSP systems. It has been developed by Salerno-based Magaldi Industries. The first commercial application of STEM was scheduled to take place in Sicily from 2015.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      CSP growth is expected to continue at a fast pace. As of January 2014, Spain had a total capacity of 2,300 MW making this country the world leader in CSP. United States follows with 1,740 MW. Interest is also notable in North Africa and the Middle East, as well as India and China. In Italy, a handful of companies are trying to get authorization for 14 plants, totalling 392 MW, despite a strong local and political opposition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The global market has been dominated by parabolic-trough plants, which account for 90% of CSP plants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saw2011_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-saw2011-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      CSP is not to be confused with
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">
       concentrator photovoltaics
      </a>
      (CPV). In CPV, the concentrated sunlight is converted directly to electricity via the
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">
       photovoltaic effect
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
       Ivanpah
      </a>
      in California is running 69 percent below advertised power output, and one Spanish company,
      <a href="/wiki/Abengoa" title="Abengoa">
       Abengoa
      </a>
      , that commercialized CSP both in the US and abroad, is teetering on the brink of bankruptcy. CSP technologies currently cannot compete on price with photovoltaics (solar panels), which have experienced huge growth in recent years due to falling prices of the panels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another drawback of Ivanpah is that it lacks thermal energy storage, one of the big advantages that CSP has over PV and most other renewables, which require either large scale energy storage systems like pumped hydro or fast acting natural gas power plants to be there as backup for those times when they are not producing much energy.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      A legend has it that
      <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">
       Archimedes
      </a>
      used a "burning glass" to concentrate sunlight on the invading Roman fleet and repel them from
      <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily#Greek_period" title="Syracuse, Sicily">
       Syracuse
      </a>
      . In 1973 a Greek scientist, Dr. Ioannis Sakkas, curious about whether Archimedes could really have destroyed the Roman fleet in 212 BC, lined up nearly 60 Greek sailors, each holding an oblong mirror tipped to catch the sun's rays and direct them at a tar-covered plywood silhouette 160 feet away. The ship caught fire after a few minutes; however, historians continue to doubt the Archimedes story.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1866,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auguste_Mouchout" title="Auguste Mouchout">
       Auguste Mouchout
      </a>
      used a parabolic trough to producе steam for the first solar steam engine. The first patent for a solar collector was obtained by the Italian Alessandro Battaglia in Genoa, Italy, in 1886. Over the following years, invеntors such as
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ericsson" title="John Ericsson">
       John Ericsson
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Shuman" title="Frank Shuman">
       Frank Shuman
      </a>
      developed concentrating solar-powered dеvices for irrigation, refrigеration, and locomоtion. In 1913 Shuman finished a 55 HP parabolic
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">
       solar thermal energy
      </a>
      station in Maadi, Egypt for irrigation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first solar-power system using a mirror dish was built by
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard" title="Robert H. Goddard">
       Dr. R.H. Goddard
      </a>
      , who was already well known for his research on liquid-fueled rockets and wrote an article in 1929 in which he asserted that all the previous obstacles had been addressed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Professor Giovanni Francia (1911–1980) designed and built the first concentrated-solar plant, which entered into operation in Sant'Ilario, near Genoa, Italy in 1968. This plant had the architecture of today's concentrated-solar plants with a solar receiver in the center of a field of solar collectors. The plant was able to produce 1 MW with superheated steam at 100 bar and 500 °C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 10 MW
      <a href="/wiki/The_Solar_Project" title="The Solar Project">
       Solar One
      </a>
      power tower was developed in Southern California in 1981, but the parabolic-trough technology of the nearby
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">
       Solar Energy Generating Systems
      </a>
      (SEGS), begun in 1984, was more workable. The 354 MW SEGS is still the largest solar power plant in the world, and will remain so until the 390 MW Ivanpah power tower project reaches full power.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_technology">
       Current technology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Current technology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      CSP is used to produce electricity (sometimes called solar thermoelectricity, usually generated through
      <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">
       steam
      </a>
      ). Concentrated-solar technology systems use
      <a href="/wiki/Mirror" title="Mirror">
       mirrors
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">
       lenses
      </a>
      with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_motion_tracking" title="Optical motion tracking">
       tracking
      </a>
      systems to focus a large area of sunlight onto a small area. The concentrated light is then used as heat or as a heat source for a conventional
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_plant" title="Power plant">
       power plant
      </a>
      (solar thermoelectricity). The solar concentrators used in CSP systems can often also be used to provide industrial process heating or cooling, such as in solar air-conditioning.
     </p>
     <p>
      Concentrating technologies exist in five common forms, namely
      <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">
       parabolic trough
      </a>
      , enclosed trough,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dish_Stirling" title="Dish Stirling">
       dish Stirlings
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_Linear_Fresnel_Reflector" title="Compact Linear Fresnel Reflector">
       concentrating linear Fresnel reflector
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_tower" title="Solar power tower">
       solar power tower
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tomkonrad_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-tomkonrad-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although simple, these solar concentrators are quite far from the theoretical maximum concentration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IntroNio2e_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IntroNio2e-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIO_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NIO-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, the parabolic-trough concentration gives about ⅓ of the theoretical maximum for the design
      <a href="/wiki/Acceptance_angle_(solar_concentrator)" title="Acceptance angle (solar concentrator)">
       acceptance angle
      </a>
      , that is, for the same overall tolerances for the system. Approaching the theoretical maximum may be achieved by using more elaborate concentrators based on
      <a href="/wiki/Nonimaging_optics" title="Nonimaging optics">
       nonimaging optics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IntroNio2e_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IntroNio2e-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIO_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NIO-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Different types of concentrators produce different peak temperatures and correspondingly varying thermodynamic efficiencies, due to differences in the way that they track the sun and focus light. New innovations in CSP technology are leading systems to become more and more cost-effective.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parabolic_trough">
       Parabolic trough
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Parabolic trough">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1887" data-file-width="2370" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg/220px-Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg/330px-Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg/440px-Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_trough_at_Harper_Lake_in_California.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Parabolic trough at a plant near Harper Lake, California
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">
       Parabolic trough
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A parabolic trough consists of a linear parabolic reflector that concentrates light onto a receiver positioned along the reflector's focal line. The receiver is a tube positioned directly above the middle of the parabolic mirror and filled with a working fluid. The reflector follows the sun during the daylight hours by tracking along a single axis. A
      <a href="/wiki/Working_fluid" title="Working fluid">
       working fluid
      </a>
      (e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt" title="Molten salt">
       molten salt
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) is heated to 150–350 °C (300–660 °F) as it flows through the receiver and is then used as a heat source for a power generation system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_2005_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Martin_2005-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Trough systems are the most developed CSP technology. The
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">
       Solar Energy Generating Systems
      </a>
      (SEGS) plants in California, the world's first commercial parabolic trough plants, Acciona's
      <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Solar_One" title="Nevada Solar One">
       Nevada Solar One
      </a>
      near
      <a href="/wiki/Boulder_City,_Nevada" title="Boulder City, Nevada">
       Boulder City, Nevada
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andasol" title="Andasol">
       Andasol
      </a>
      , Europe's first commercial parabolic trough plant are representative, along with
      <a href="/wiki/Plataforma_Solar_de_Almer%C3%ADa" title="Plataforma Solar de Almería">
       Plataforma Solar de Almería
      </a>
      's SSPS-DCS test facilities in
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Spain" title="Solar power in Spain">
       Spain
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plataforma_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Plataforma-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Enclosed_trough">
       Enclosed trough
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Enclosed trough">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Enclosed trough systems are used to absorb heat rather than
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Process_heat" title="Process heat">
       process heat
      </a>
      . The design encapsulates the solar thermal system within a greenhouse-like glasshouse. The glasshouse creates a protected environment to withstand the elements that can negatively impact reliability and efficiency of the solar thermal system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deloitte_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-deloitte-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lightweight curved solar-reflecting mirrors are suspended from the ceiling of the glasshouse by wires. A
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">
       single-axis tracking system
      </a>
      positions the mirrors to retrieve the optimal amount of sunlight. The mirrors concentrate the sunlight and focus it on a network of stationary steel pipes, also suspended from the glasshouse structure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Water is carried throughout the length of the pipe, which is boiled to generate steam when intense solar radiation is applied. Sheltering the mirrors from the wind allows them to achieve higher temperature rates and prevents dust from building up on the mirrors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deloitte_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-deloitte-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3745" data-file-width="5617" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg/220px-Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg/330px-Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg/440px-Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inside_an_enclosed_CSP_Trough.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Inside an enclosed trough system
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/GlassPoint_Solar" title="GlassPoint Solar">
       GlassPoint Solar
      </a>
      , the company that created the Enclosed Trough design, states its technology can produce heat for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Oil_Recovery" title="Enhanced Oil Recovery">
       Enhanced Oil Recovery
      </a>
      (EOR) for about $5 per million British thermal units in sunny regions, compared to between $10 and $12 for other conventional solar thermal technologies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehren_chevron_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehren_chevron-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_power_tower">
       Solar power tower
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Solar power tower">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_tower" title="Solar power tower">
       Solar power tower
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="700" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg/300px-PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg/450px-PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg/600px-PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PS10_solar_power_tower.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/PS10_solar_power_plant" title="PS10 solar power plant">
         PS10 solar power plant
        </a>
        in Andalucía, Spain, concentrates sunlight from a field of
        <a href="/wiki/Heliostat" title="Heliostat">
         heliostats
        </a>
        onto a central
        <a href="#Solar_power_tower">
         solar power tower
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A solar power tower consists of an array of dual-axis tracking reflectors (
      <a href="/wiki/Heliostat" title="Heliostat">
       heliostats
      </a>
      ) that concentrate sunlight on a central receiver atop a tower; the receiver contains a fluid deposit, which can consist of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_water" title="Sea water">
       sea water
      </a>
      . The working fluid in the receiver is heated to 500–1000 °C (773–1,273 K (932–1,832 °F)) and then used as a heat source for a power generation or energy storage system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_2005_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Martin_2005-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An advantage of the solar tower is the reflectors can be adjusted instead of the whole tower. Power-tower development is less advanced than trough systems, but they offer higher efficiency and better energy storage capability. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Two" title="Solar Two">
       Solar Two
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Daggett,_California" title="Daggett, California">
       Daggett
      </a>
      , California and the CESA-1 in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plataforma_Solar_de_Almeria" title="Plataforma Solar de Almeria">
       Plataforma Solar de Almeria
      </a>
      Almeria, Spain, are the most representative demonstration plants. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PS10_solar_power_tower" title="PS10 solar power tower">
       Planta Solar 10
      </a>
      (PS10) in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanlucar_la_Mayor" title="Sanlucar la Mayor">
       Sanlucar la Mayor
      </a>
      , Spain, is the first commercial utility-scale solar power tower in the world. The 377 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
       Ivanpah Solar Power Facility
      </a>
      , located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">
       Mojave Desert
      </a>
      , is the largest CSP facility in the world, and uses three power towers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="external text" href="http://energy.sandia.gov/?page_id=1267" rel="nofollow">
       National Solar Thermal Test Facility, NSTTF
      </a>
      located in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_NM" title="Albuquerque, NM">
       Albuquerque, NM
      </a>
      , is an experimental solar thermal test facility with a heliostat field capable of producing 6 MW.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fresnel_reflectors">
       Fresnel reflectors
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fresnel reflectors">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_Linear_Fresnel_Reflector" title="Compact Linear Fresnel Reflector">
       Compact Linear Fresnel Reflector
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Fresnel reflectors are made of many thin, flat mirror strips to concentrate sunlight onto tubes through which working fluid is pumped. Flat mirrors allow more reflective surface in the same amount of space as a parabolic reflector, thus capturing more of the available sunlight, and they are much cheaper than parabolic reflectors. Fresnel reflectors can be used in various size CSPs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dish_Stirling">
       Dish Stirling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dish Stirling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SolarStirlingEngine.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="360" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/SolarStirlingEngine.jpg/220px-SolarStirlingEngine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/SolarStirlingEngine.jpg/330px-SolarStirlingEngine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/SolarStirlingEngine.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SolarStirlingEngine.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy#Dish_designs" title="Solar thermal energy">
         A dish Stirling
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dish_Stirling" title="Dish Stirling">
       Dish Stirling
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A dish Stirling or dish engine system consists of a stand-alone
      <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">
       parabolic reflector
      </a>
      that concentrates light onto a receiver positioned at the reflector's focal point. The reflector tracks the Sun along two axes. The working fluid in the receiver is heated to 250–700 °C (480–1,300 °F) and then used by a
      <a href="/wiki/Stirling_engine" title="Stirling engine">
       Stirling engine
      </a>
      to generate power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_2005_26-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Martin_2005-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parabolic-dish systems provide high solar-to-electric efficiency (between 31% and 32%), and their modular nature provides scalability. The
      <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Energy_Systems" title="Stirling Energy Systems">
       Stirling Energy Systems
      </a>
      (SES),
      <a href="/wiki/United_Sun_Systems_International" title="United Sun Systems International">
       United Sun Systems
      </a>
      (USS) and
      <a href="/wiki/Science_Applications_International_Corporation" title="Science Applications International Corporation">
       Science Applications International Corporation (SAIC)
      </a>
      dishes at
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nevada,_Las_Vegas" title="University of Nevada, Las Vegas">
       UNLV
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_National_University" title="Australian National University">
       Australian National University
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Dish_(solar_thermal)" title="The Big Dish (solar thermal)">
       Big Dish
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">
       Canberra
      </a>
      , Australia are representative of this technology. A world record for solar to electric efficiency was set at 31.25% by SES dishes at the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Solar_Thermal_Test_Facility" title="National Solar Thermal Test Facility">
       National Solar Thermal Test Facility
      </a>
      (NSTTF) in New Mexico on January 31, 2008, a cold, bright day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to its developer,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ripasso_Energy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ripasso Energy (page does not exist)">
       Ripasso Energy
      </a>
      , a Swedish firm, in 2015 its Dish Sterling system being tested in the
      <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">
       Kalahari Desert
      </a>
      in South Africa showed 34% efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian51315_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Guardian51315-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The SES installation in Maricopa, Phoenix was the largest Stirling Dish power installation in the world until it was sold to
      <a href="/wiki/United_Sun_Systems_International" title="United Sun Systems International">
       United Sun Systems
      </a>
      . Subsequently, larger parts of the installation have been moved to China as part of the huge energy demand.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Large_Air_Mass_Solar_Power">
       Large Air Mass Solar Power
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Large Air Mass Solar Power">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A concentrated solar power process developed in 2015 whereby a the temperature of a large contained mass of air is varied by a relatively small amounts. This method has the potential to be an extremely efficient way to both harness and store solar power, since the expansive and contractive forces generated by the large air mass can be directly used to fill reservoirs. This process could make practical the construction of coastal reservoirs powered by seawater.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_thermal_enhanced_oil_recovery">
       Solar thermal enhanced oil recovery
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Solar thermal enhanced oil recovery">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Solar thermal enhanced oil recovery">
       Solar thermal enhanced oil recovery
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Heat from the sun can be used to provide steam used to make heavy oil less viscous and easier to pump. Solar power tower and parabolic troughs can be used to provide the steam which is used directly so no generators are required and no electricity is produced. Solar thermal enhanced oil recovery can extend the life of oilfields with very thick oil which would not otherwise be economical to pump.
     </p>

     <p>
      The commercial deployment of CSP plants started by 1984 in the US with the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">
       SEGS
      </a>
      plants. The last SEGS plant was completed in 1990. From 1991 to 2005 no CSP plants were built anywhere in the world. Global installed CSP-capacity has increased nearly tenfold since 2004 and grew at an average of 50 percent per year during the last five years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren21-gsr-2014_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ren21-gsr-2014-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :51
      </sup>
      In 2013, worldwide installed capacity increased by 36 percent or nearly 0.9
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">
       gigawatt
      </a>
      (GW) to more than 3.4 GW.
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Spain" title="Solar power in Spain">
       Spain
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">
       United States
      </a>
      remained the global leaders, while the number of countries with installed CSP were growing. There is a notable trend towards developing countries and regions with high solar radiation.
     </p>
     <p>
      CSP is also increasingly competing with the cheaper
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">
       photovoltaic
      </a>
      solar power and with
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">
       concentrator photovoltaics
      </a>
      (CPV), a fast-growing technology that just like CSP is suited best for regions of high solar insolation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, a novel solar CPV/CSP hybrid system has been proposed recently.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">
       Efficiency
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Efficiency">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The conversion efficiency
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             η
             <!-- η -->
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \eta }
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\eta " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e4d701857cf5fbec133eebaf94deadf722537f64" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.18ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      of the incident solar radiation into mechanical work − without considering the ultimate conversion step into electricity by a power generator − depends on the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">
       thermal radiation
      </a>
      properties of the solar receiver and on the heat engine (e.g. steam turbine). Solar irradiation is first converted into heat by the solar receiver with the efficiency
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <msub>
             <mi>
              η
              <!-- η -->
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>
               R
              </mi>
              <mi>
               e
              </mi>
              <mi>
               c
              </mi>
              <mi>
               e
              </mi>
              <mi>
               i
              </mi>
              <mi>
               v
              </mi>
              <mi>
               e
              </mi>
              <mi>
               r
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </msub>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \eta _{Receiver}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\eta _{{Receiver}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5a55d1fd3d0c71574e61ac1f1d94789f8d6453e" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.821ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      and subsequently the heat is converted into work by the heat engine with the efficiency
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <msub>
             <mi>
              η
              <!-- η -->
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>
               C
              </mi>
              <mi>
               a
              </mi>
              <mi>
               r
              </mi>
              <mi>
               n
              </mi>
              <mi>
               o
              </mi>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </msub>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \eta _{Carnot}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\eta _{{Carnot}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/62c89de24c38c6cddc34bb2654b16ff77f29e497" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.68ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      , using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnot%27s_principle" title="Carnot's principle">
       Carnot's principle
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For a solar receiver providing a heat source at temperature T
      <sub>
       H
      </sub>
      and a heat sink at room temperature T°, the overall conversion efficiency can be calculated as follows:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

             </mi>

             </mo>

               <!-- η -->
              </mi>

           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \eta =\eta _{\mathrm {Receiver} }\cdot \eta _{\mathrm {Carnot} }}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\eta =\eta _{{\mathrm  {Receiver}}}\cdot \eta _{{\mathrm  {Carnot}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6dbb17cb629fd80a44998a1ba5c25cdcce8d28bd" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:20.007ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
      <dd>
       with
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>

                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              1
             </mn>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mfrac>
               <msup>
                <mi>
                 T
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mn>
                  0
                 </mn>
                </mrow>
               </msup>
               <msub>
                <mi>
                 T
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi>
                  H
                 </mi>
                </mrow>

              </mfrac>
             </mrow>

         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\eta _{{\mathrm  {Carnot}}}=1-{\frac  {T^{0}}{T_{H}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1c2cfd63737654d168a7307aff89130eee84272b" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:17.507ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
      <dd>
       and
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>

             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mfrac>
               <mrow>
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  Q
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                <mi>
                 Q

                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

            {\displaystyle \eta _{\mathrm {Receiver} }={\frac {Q_{\mathrm {absorbed} }-Q_{\mathrm {lost} }}{Q_{\mathrm {solar} }}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\eta _{{\mathrm  {Receiver}}}={\frac  {Q_{{\mathrm  {absorbed}}}-Q_{{\mathrm  {lost}}}}{Q_{{\mathrm  {solar}}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/efa7aa34402d27dcfee38dbfc58dd27b47f70b93" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:27.579ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         where
         <span>
          <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
           <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
            <semantics>

         </span>
         ,
         <span>
          <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
           <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

          <img alt="Q_{{\mathrm  {lost}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3212eb9b95d080171a7ec174643518065c162cd9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.678ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
         </span>
         are respectively the incoming
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flux" title="Solar flux">
          solar flux
         </a>
         and the fluxes absorbed and lost by the system solar receiver.
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      For a solar flux
      <i>
       I
      </i>
      (e.g.
      <i>
       I
      </i>
      = 1000 W/m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) concentrated
      <i>
       C
      </i>
      times with an efficiency
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <msub>
             <mi>
              η
              <!-- η -->
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>
               O
              </mi>
              <mi>
               p
              </mi>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mi>
               i
              </mi>

      </span>

      </span>
      = 1) then substituting in the first equation gives

     <p>
      <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_concentration_efficiency.png" title="Maximum solar-to-work efficiency for a simplified solar receiver relative to the temperature for various concentrations">
       <img alt="Maximum solar-to-work efficiency for a simplified solar receiver relative to the temperature for various concentrations" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="1029" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Solar_concentration_efficiency.png/400px-Solar_concentration_efficiency.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Solar_concentration_efficiency.png/600px-Solar_concentration_efficiency.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Solar_concentration_efficiency.png/800px-Solar_concentration_efficiency.png 2x" width="400"/>
      </a>
     </p>
     <p>
      The graph shows that the overall efficiency does not increase steadily with the receiver's temperature. Although the heat engine's efficiency (Carnot) increases with higher temperature, the receiver's efficiency does not. On the contrary, the receiver's efficiency is decreasing, as the amount of energy it cannot absorb (Q
      <sub>
       lost
      </sub>
      ) grows by the fourth power as a function of temperature. Hence, there is a maximum reachable temperature. When the receiver efficiency is null (blue curve on the figure below), T
      <sub>
       max
      </sub>
      is:
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                a
               </mi>
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                x
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </mrow>
            </msub>
            <mo>
             =
            </mo>
            <msup>
             <mrow>
              <mo>
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  I
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  C
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mi>
                 σ
                 <!-- σ -->
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               )
              </mo>
             </mrow>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mn>
               0.25
              </mn>
             </mrow>
            </msup>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle T_{\mathrm {max} }=\left({\frac {IC}{\sigma }}\right)^{0.25}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="T_{{\mathrm  {max}}}=\left({{\frac  {IC}{\sigma }}}\right)^{{0.25}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f13c71e070bf1d6bc53031aadd7816912de95f52" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:18.213ex; height:6.676ex;"/>
      </span>
     </p>
     <p>
      There is a temperature T
      <sub>
       opt
      </sub>
      for which the efficiency is maximum, i.e. when the efficiency derivative relative to the receiver temperature is null:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

               <mrow>
                <mi>
                 d
                </mi>

                 <!-- η -->
                </mi>
               </mrow>

                </mi>

             </mo>

        </span>

               </mrow>
               <mrow>
                <mn>
                 4
                </mn>
                <mi>
                 σ
                 <!-- σ -->
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mfrac>
             </mrow>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle T_{opt}^{5}-(0.75T^{0})T_{\mathrm {opt} }^{4}-{\frac {T^{0}IC}{4\sigma }}=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="T_{{opt}}^{5}-(0.75T^{0})T_{{\mathrm  {opt}}}^{4}-{\frac  {T^{0}IC}{4\sigma }}=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e10cdad89d8d8d28a380290ef6fe6b67628dae5d" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:33.207ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Solving this equation numerically allows us to obtain the optimum process temperature according to the solar concentration ratio
      <i>
       C
      </i>
      (red curve on the figure below)
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png" title="Maximum (top, blue) and optimum (bottom, red) temperatures for a solar receiver relative to its concentration ratio">
       <img alt="Maximum (top, blue) and optimum (bottom, red) temperatures for a solar receiver relative to its concentration ratio" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="1211" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png/400px-SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png/600px-SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png/800px-SolarConcentration_max_opt_temperatures.png 2x" width="400"/>
      </a>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; width: 25%;">
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        C
       </th>
       <td>
        500
       </td>
       <td>
        1000
       </td>
       <td>
        5000
       </td>
       <td>
        10000
       </td>
       <td>
        45000 (max. for Earth)
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        T
        <sub>
         max
        </sub>
       </th>
       <td>
        1720
       </td>
       <td>
        2050
       </td>
       <td>
        3060
       </td>
       <td>
        3640
       </td>
       <td>
        5300
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th scope="row">
        T
        <sub>
         opt
        </sub>
       </th>
       <td>
        970
       </td>
       <td>
        1100
       </td>
