       </td>
       <td>
        1720
       </td>
       <td>
        2310
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Theoretical efficiencies aside, real-world experience of CSP reveals a 25-60 percent shortfall in projected production. A pilot 5 megawatt CSP power tower,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Solar_Project#Solar_One" title="The Solar Project">
       Solar One
      </a>
      , was converted to a 10 megawatt CSP power tower,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Solar_Project#Solar_Two" title="The Solar Project">
       Solar Two
      </a>
      , decommissioned in 1999. Due to the success of Solar Two, a commercial power plant, called
      <a href="/wiki/Gemasolar_Thermosolar_Plant" title="Gemasolar Thermosolar Plant">
       Solar Tres Power Tower
      </a>
      , was built in Spain, renamed Gemasolar Thermosolar Plant. Gemasolar brilliant results have paved the way for the
      <a href="/wiki/Crescent_Dunes_Solar_Energy_Project" title="Crescent Dunes Solar Energy Project">
       Crescent Dunes project
      </a>
      . Ivanpah difficulties arise also from not having considered the lessons about the benefits of thermal storage.
      <a href="/wiki/Solana_Generating_Station" title="Solana Generating Station">
       Solana
      </a>
      in Arizona is 25 percent below projected numbers, Ivanpah in California, is 40 percent below projected numbers. A slightly bigger
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">
       photovoltaic power station
      </a>
      , like the 290 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Agua_Caliente_Solar_Project" title="Agua Caliente Solar Project">
       Agua Caliente Solar Project
      </a>
      peaked at most to 741 GW·h in 2014, comparing with the 280 MW Solana growing 719 GW·h. Lower insolation related to a solar minimum is part of the problem, but there is clearly a technical issue with rating these systems or simply a commercial overstatement. Indeed, an other operator, that of the 280 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Genesis_Solar_Energy_Project" title="Genesis Solar Energy Project">
       Genesis Solar
      </a>
      , projected only 580 GW·h production and instead made 621 GW·h in 2015. CSP once thought to be economically superior to photovoltaics, in 2015 it has proven not to be the case. Recent PV commercial power is selling for ⅓ recent CSP contracts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">
       Costs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Costs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      As of 9 September 2009
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , the cost of building a CSP station was typically about US$2.50 to $4 per watt,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Mirror_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Google_Mirror-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while the fuel (the sun's radiation) is free. Thus a 250 MW CSP station would have cost $600–1000 million to build. That works out to $0.12 to
      <span class="nowrap">
       0.18 USD/kWh.
      </span>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Mirror_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Google_Mirror-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      New CSP stations may be economically competitive with fossil fuels. Nathaniel Bullard, a solar analyst at Bloomberg New Energy Finance, has calculated that the cost of electricity at the
      <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
       Ivanpah Solar Power Facility
      </a>
      , a project under construction in Southern California, will be lower than that from photovoltaic power and about the same as that from natural gas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, in November 2011, Google announced that they would not invest further in CSP projects due to the rapid price decline of
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
       photovoltaics
      </a>
      . Google invested US$168 million on BrightSource.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-googone_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-googone-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/International_Renewable_Energy_Agency" title="International Renewable Energy Agency">
       IRENA
      </a>
      has published on June 2012 a series of studies titled: "Renewable Energy Cost Analysis". The CSP study shows the cost of both building and operation of CSP plants. Costs are expected to decrease, but there are insufficient installations to clearly establish the learning curve. As of March 2012, there were 1.9 GW of CSP installed, with 1.8 GW of that being parabolic trough.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRENA_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IRENA-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Incentives">
       Incentives
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Incentives">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">
       Spain
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Spain">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Solar-thermal electricity generation is eligible for feed-in tariff payments (art. 2 RD 661/2007), if the system capacity does not exceed the following limits: Systems registered in the register of systems prior to 29 September 2008: 500 MW for solar-thermal systems. Systems registered after 29 September 2008 (PV only). The capacity limits for the different system types are re-defined during the review of the application conditions every quarter (art. 5 RD 1578/2008, Annex III RD 1578/2008). Prior to the end of an application period, the market caps specified for each system type are published on the website of the Ministry of Industry, Tourism and Trade (art. 5 RD 1578/2008).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since 27 January 2012, Spain has halted acceptance of new projects for the feed-in-tariff.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Projects currently accepted are not affected, except that a 6% tax on feed-in-tariffs has been adopted, effectively reducing the feed-in-tariff.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">
       Australia
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Australia">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_Australia" title="Energy policy of Australia">
       Energy policy of Australia
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      At the federal level, under the Large-scale Renewable Energy Target (LRET), in operation under the Renewable Energy Electricity Act 2000, large scale solar thermal electricity generation from accredited RET power stations may be entitled to create large-scale generation certificates (LGCs). These certificates can then be sold and transferred to liable entities (usually electricity retailers) to meet their obligations under this tradeable certificates scheme. However, as this legislation is technology neutral in its operation, it tends to favour more established RE technologies with a lower levelised cost of generation, such as large scale onshore wind, rather than solar thermal and CSP.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At State level, renewable energy feed-in laws typically are capped by maximum generation capacity in kWp, and are open only to micro or medium scale generation and in a number of instances are only open to solar PV (photovoltaic) generation. This means that larger scale CSP projects would not be eligible for payment for feed-in incentives in many of the State and Territory jurisdictions.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Future">
       Future
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Future">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      A study done by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenpeace_International" title="Greenpeace International">
       Greenpeace International
      </a>
      , the European Solar Thermal Electricity Association, and the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/SolarPACES" title="SolarPACES">
       SolarPACES
      </a>
      group investigated the potential and future of concentrated solar power. The study found that concentrated solar power could account for up to 25% of the world's energy needs by 2050. The increase in investment would be from 2 billion euros worldwide to 92.5 billion euros in that time period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-guardian-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spain is the leader in concentrated solar power technology, with more than 50 government-approved projects in the works. Also, it exports its technology, further increasing the technology's stake in energy worldwide. Because the technology works best with areas of high
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">
       insolation
      </a>
      (solar radiation), experts predict the biggest growth in places like Africa, Mexico, and the southwest United States. It indicates that the thermal storage systems based in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrates" title="Nitrates">
       nitrates
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_nitrate" title="Calcium nitrate">
       calcium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">
       potassium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">
       sodium
      </a>
      ,...) will make the CSP plants more and more profitable. The study examined three different outcomes for this technology: no increases in CSP technology, investment continuing as it has been in Spain and the US, and finally the true potential of CSP without any barriers on its growth. The findings of the third part are shown in the table below:
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: right; width: 330px;">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Year
       </th>
       <th>
        Annual
        <br/>
        Investment
       </th>
       <th>
        Cumulative
        <br/>
        Capacity
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td align="center">
        2015
       </td>
       <td>
        21 billion euros
       </td>
       <td>
        420 megawatts
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td align="center">
        2050
       </td>
       <td>
        174 billion euros
       </td>
       <td>
        1,500,000 megawatts
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Finally, the study acknowledged how technology for CSP was improving and how this would result in a drastic price decrease by 2050. It predicted a drop from the current range of €0.23–0.15/kwh to €0.14–0.10/kwh.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_57-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-guardian-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recently the EU has begun to look into developing a €400 billion ($774 billion) network of solar power plants based in the Sahara region using CSP technology known as
      <a href="/wiki/Desertec" title="Desertec">
       Desertec
      </a>
      , to create "a new carbon-free network linking Europe, the Middle East and North Africa". The plan is backed mainly by German industrialists and predicts production of 15% of Europe's power by 2050.
      <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
       Morocco
      </a>
      is a major partner in Desertec and as it has barely 1% of the electricity consumption of the EU, it will produce more than enough energy for the entire country with a large energy surplus to deliver to Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">
       Algeria
      </a>
      has the biggest area of desert, and private Algerian firm
      <a href="/wiki/Cevital" title="Cevital">
       Cevital
      </a>
      has signed up for
      <a href="/wiki/Desertec" title="Desertec">
       Desertec
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_58-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      With its wide desert (the highest CSP potential in the Mediterranean and Middle East regions ~ about 170 TWh/year) and its strategic geographical location near Europe
      <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">
       Algeria
      </a>
      is one of the key countries to ensure the success of Desertec project. Moreover, with the abundant natural-gas reserve in the Algerian desert, this will strengthen the technical potential of Algeria in acquiring
      <a href="/wiki/Hassi_R%27Mel_integrated_solar_combined_cycle_power_station" title="Hassi R'Mel integrated solar combined cycle power station">
       Solar-Gas Hybrid Power Plants
      </a>
      for 24-hour electricity generation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Other organizations expect CSP to cost $0.06(US)/kWh by 2015 due to efficiency improvements and mass production of equipment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      That would make CSP as cheap as conventional power. Investors such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venture_capitalist" title="Venture capitalist">
       venture capitalist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Vinod_Khosla" title="Vinod Khosla">
       Vinod Khosla
      </a>
      expect CSP to continuously reduce costs and actually be cheaper than coal power after 2015.
     </p>
     <p>
      On 9 September 2009
      <span class="noprint">
       ; 7 years ago
      </span>
      <span style="display:none">
       (
       <span class="bday dtstart published updated">
        2009-09-09
       </span>
       )
      </span>
      , Bill Weihl,
      <a href="/wiki/Google.org" title="Google.org">
       Google.org
      </a>
      's green-energy spokesperson said that the firm was conducting research on the heliostat mirrors and gas turbine technology, which he expects will drop the cost of solar thermal electric power to less than $0.05/kWh in 2 or 3 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Google_Mirror_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Google_Mirror-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2009, scientists at the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">
       National Renewable Energy Laboratory
      </a>
      (NREL) and
      <a href="/wiki/SkyFuel" title="SkyFuel">
       SkyFuel
      </a>
      teamed to develop large curved sheets of metal that have the potential to be 30% less expensive than today's best collectors of concentrated solar power by replacing glass-based models with a
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      polymer sheet that has the same performance as the heavy glass mirrors, but at much lower cost and weight. It also is much easier to deploy and install. The glossy film uses several layers of polymers, with an inner layer of pure silver.
     </p>
     <p>
      Telescope designer
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_Angel" title="Roger Angel">
       Roger Angel
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Univ._of_Arizona" title="Univ. of Arizona">
       Univ. of Arizona
      </a>
      ) has turned his attention to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics" title="Concentrated photovoltaics">
       CPV
      </a>
      , and is a partner in a company called Rehnu. Angel utilizes a spherical concentrating lens with large-telescope technologies, but much cheaper materials and mechanisms, to create efficient systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recent experience with CSP technology in 2014 - 2015 at Solana in Arizona, and Ivanpah in Nevada indicate large production shortfalls in electricity generation between 25 and 40 percent. Producers blame clouds and stormy weather, but critics seem to think there are technological issues. These problems are causing utilities to pay inflated prices for wholesale electricity, and threaten the long-term viability of the technology. As photovoltaic costs continue to plummet, many think CSP has a limited future in utility-scale electricity production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the other side, part of photovoltaic costs drop is the result of the fossil fuel cost reductions, which still powers most of PV production. Instead CSP would be unaffected when fossil prices return to higher quotes.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Very_large_scale_solar_power_plants">
       Very large scale solar power plants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Very large scale solar power plants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      There are several proposals for gigawatt size, very large scale solar power plants. They include the Euro-Mediterranean
      <a href="/wiki/Desertec" title="Desertec">
       Desertec
      </a>
      proposal, Project Helios in Greece (10 gigawatt), and Ordos (2 gigawatt) in China. A 2003 study concluded that the world could generate 2,357,840 TWh each year from very large scale solar power plants using 1% of each of the world's deserts. Total consumption worldwide was 15,223 TWh/year
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (in 2003). The gigawatt size projects are arrays of single plants. The largest single plant in operation is the 370 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
       Ivanpah Solar
      </a>
      . In 2012, the
      <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Land_Management" title="Bureau of Land Management">
       BLM
      </a>
      made available 97,921,069 acres (39,627,251 hectares) of land in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">
       southwestern United States
      </a>
      for solar projects, enough for between 10,000 and 20,000 gigawatts (GW).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_wildlife">
       Effect on wildlife
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Concentrated_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Effect on wildlife">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      It has been noted that insects can be attracted to the bright light caused by concentrated solar technology, and as a result birds that hunt them can be killed (burned) if the birds fly near the point where light is being focused onto. This can also affect raptors who hunt the birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Federal wildlife officials have begun calling these power towers "mega traps" for wildlife.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, the story about the
      <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
       Ivanpah Solar Power Facility
      </a>
      was exaggerated, numbering the deaths in many tens of thousands, spreading alarm about concentrated solar power (CSP) plants, which was not grounded in facts, but on one opponent's speculation. According to rigorous reporting, in over six months, actually only 133 singed birds were counted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By focusing no more than 4 mirrors on any one place in the air during standby, at
      <a href="/wiki/Crescent_Dunes_Solar_Energy_Project" title="Crescent Dunes Solar Energy Project">
       Crescent Dunes Solar Energy Project
      </a>
      , in 3 months, the death rate dropped to zero fatalities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
